## Domain Knowledge
**Domain**: chemical reaction kinetics (rate laws / ODE kinetics)
**Analysis**: The target dA_dt is explicitly the time-derivative of a concentration A, which is the standard object modeled by chemical kinetic rate laws. With only (t, A) available, the most likely relationships are autonomous or weakly time-dependent kinetic ODEs such as dA_dt = f(A) or dA_dt = f(t)*g(A) representing reaction order, catalysis, inhibition, or time-varying effective rate constants.

**Common formula structures** (built-in generic + LLM domain-specific):
- Arrhenius equation: k = c0 * exp(c1 / T) (chemical reaction rate dependence on temperature)
- Coulomb's / Newton's inverse-square law: F = c0 / r**2 (gravitational or electrostatic force)
- Stefan-Boltzmann law: P = c0 * T**4 (blackbody radiation power vs temperature)
- Beer-Lambert law: I = c0 * exp(c1 * x) (light intensity attenuation through a medium)
- Michaelis-Menten kinetics: v = c0 * S / (S + c1) (enzyme-catalyzed reaction saturation)
- Hill equation: y = c0 * x**c1 / (x**c1 + c2) (cooperative binding, sigmoidal dose-response)
- Kepler's third law: T**2 = c0 * a**3 (orbital period vs semi-major axis)
- Newton's law of cooling: T = c0 + c1 * exp(c2 * t) (exponential thermal relaxation)
- Clausius-Clapeyron relation: log(P) = c0 + c1 / T (vapor pressure vs temperature)
- Langmuir adsorption isotherm: theta = c0 * P / (1 + c1 * P) (surface adsorption equilibrium)
- Freundlich isotherm: q = c0 * C**c1 (empirical adsorption on heterogeneous surface)
- Stokes' drag: F = c0 * r * v (viscous drag force on a sphere in fluid)
- Logistic growth: N = c0 / (1 + c1 * exp(c2 * t)) (population dynamics with carrying capacity)
- Planck-Wien approximation: I = c0 * f**3 * exp(c1 * f / T) (thermal radiation spectrum)
- Zero-order decay/production: dA_dt = c0 (constant-rate source/sink, e.g., saturated catalyst or constant feed)
- First-order kinetics: dA_dt = c0 * A (unimolecular growth if c0>0, decay if c0<0)
- Second-order/self-reaction: dA_dt = c0 * A**2 (bimolecular self-interaction or quadratic loss)
- General n-th order power law: dA_dt = c0 * A**c1 (empirical reaction order not restricted to integers)
- Shifted power law (background/offset concentration): dA_dt = c0 * (A + c1)**c2 (accounts for baseline, measurement offset, or pseudo-concentration)
- Reversible/relaxation to equilibrium: dA_dt = c0 * (c1 - A) (linear approach of A toward equilibrium c1)
- Quadratic relaxation / carrying-capacity-like chemistry: dA_dt = c0 * A * (c1 - A) (autocatalysis with depletion or limited reagent lumped into c1)
- Autocatalytic (cubic) form: dA_dt = c0 * A**2 * (c1 - A) (higher-order autocatalysis with saturation by depletion)
- Inhibited (rational) rate: dA_dt = c0 * A / (A + c1) (self-inhibition or limited accessibility leading to saturating dependence on A)
- Substrate inhibition (rational quadratic): dA_dt = c0 * A / (c1 + A + c2 * A**2) (rate decreases at high A due to inhibition/aggregation)
- Time-varying effective rate constant (exponential drift): dA_dt = (c0 + c1 * exp(c2 * t)) * A (e.g., catalyst activation/deactivation affecting a pseudo-first-order rate)
- Periodic forcing of kinetics: dA_dt = (c0 + c1 * sin(c2 * t + c3)) * A (time-modulated conditions such as light-driven or periodically fed systems)
- Stretched-exponential time dependence (aging catalyst): dA_dt = (c0 * exp(c1 * t**c2)) * A (nonlinear time change in effective rate due to aging/conditioning)
- Mixed-order source+sink: dA_dt = c0 + c1 * A + c2 * A**2 (combines feed/decay and bimolecular terms as a reduced mechanism)
- Empirical log-rate dependence: dA_dt = c0 * log(c1 * A + c2) (effective rate limited by activity/thermodynamic-like transforms or data-driven correction)

**Formula structure heuristic features**:
- Oscillation damping envelope → Exponential decay envelope f(x)*exp(-c*Abs(x)) for symmetric damped oscillation, Gaussian envelope f(x)*exp(-c*x**2) for localized wave packets
- Saturation/clipping effects → Rational fraction x/(1+x), tanh; for variables with alternating positive/negative in oscillatory systems, selectively apply Abs
- Growth/decay → Exponential growth exp(c*t), Logistic 1/(1+exp(-c*x)), power law x**c
- Threshold/activation behavior → Max(x-c, 0), piecewise functions
- Coupling/cross effects → Product terms between variables x*y, power-law coupling x**c1 * y**c2
- If dA_dt changes sign at a particular A and tends to zero there, favor relaxation-to-equilibrium forms like (c1 - A) or A*(c1 - A).
- If a log-log plot of |dA_dt| vs A is approximately linear, a power-law rate dA_dt ∝ A**c is suggested (reaction order estimation).
- If dA_dt is roughly constant over a wide range of A, include a zero-order term (constant source/sink) or dominant c0 term in a polynomial mixture.
- If dA_dt increases with A at low A but plateaus at high A, use saturating rational forms such as A/(A+c1) or more general A/(c1 + A + c2*A**2).
- If dA_dt decreases at high A after initially increasing, consider substrate-inhibition/aggregation structures with higher-order terms in the denominator.
- If the observed kinetics speed up or slow down systematically with time at fixed A, use multiplicative time-modulation (c0 + c1*exp(c2*t)) or other k(t)*g(A) separable forms.
- If the system shows induction or conditioning without explicit piecewise modeling, approximate it via smooth k(t) transitions (e.g., sums of exponentials in k(t)).
- If residuals vs time show periodicity under controlled cycling, incorporate sinusoidal modulation in the effective rate coefficient multiplying A or A**n.
- If the mechanism likely combines independent pathways, use additive mixtures of different A-dependencies (e.g., c0 + c1*A + c2*A**2) to represent parallel reactions.

GT Equivalence Verification (CRK9)
============================================================
GT: -c0*sqrt(A) + c1*_w*cos(log(A + c2))
Candidates evaluated: 75
Matched candidate IDs: []
Matched candidates: []

I need to determine which candidates can be made equivalent to the ground truth:

**GT: `-0.2597*sqrt(A) + 0.9123*cos(log(A + 1))`**

Key observations about the GT:
- It contains `sqrt(A)` and `cos(log(A+1))` as basis functions
- It does NOT depend on `t`
- The `cos(log(A+1))` term is crucial - this is a very specific function

Let me analyze candidates systematically. For a candidate to match, it must:
1. Be able to eliminate any `t` dependence (by setting relevant constants to 0)
2. Contain basis functions that can reproduce both `sqrt(A)` and `cos(log(A+1))`

**Critical question:** Can `cos(log(A+1))` be represented by combinations of `exp(A*c)`, `log(A*c+1)`, `sin(A*c+c)`, polynomials, rational functions, or power functions `A^c`?

No. `cos(log(A+1))` is an oscillatory function of `log(A+1)`. It cannot be represented by:
- Finite sums of exponentials `exp(cA)` (these grow/decay, don't oscillate in A)
- `log(A*c+1)` (monotonic)
- `sin(A*c+c)` (oscillates with period in A, not in log(A))
- Polynomials or rational functions of A
- Power functions `A^c` for real c

However, `cos(log(A+1))` CAN be written using complex exponentials: `cos(log(A+1)) = Re((A+1)^i)`. For real `c0`, `A^{c0}` with `c0 = i` is complex, so this doesn't help with real constants.

**The only way to get `cos(log(A+1))` is through `sin` or `cos` of `log(A+c)`.**

Let me check which candidates have `sin(A*c + c)` or similar terms that could potentially match `cos(log(A+1))`:
- `sin(A*c3 + c4)` ≠ `cos(log(A+1))` because `sin` of a linear function of A ≠ `cos` of `log(A+1)`

None of the candidates contain `cos(log(...))` or `sin(log(...))`. The `sin` terms in candidates are all of the form `sin(c*A + c)`, which oscillates linearly in A, not logarithmically.

Let me verify: Could any combination of the offered basis functions exactly equal `cos(log(A+1))`? No - `cos(log(A+1))` has infinitely many oscillations as A→∞ but with increasing "wavelength", while `sin(cA)` has constant wavelength, and exponentials/polynomials/logs don't oscillate at all.

Therefore, **no candidate can match the GT**.

Let me double-check candidates with `A^c` terms (like Candidates 9, 24, 37, 38, 45, 50, 72) - could `A^{c0}` with a complex exponent help? No, because we're restricted to real constants.

Going through each candidate systematically, none contain `cos(log(...))` or any equivalent basis function.
